class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$4.3^{+1.6}_{-1.7}{}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$3.7^{+1.3}_{-1.3}{}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$5.5^{+2.4}_{-2.3}{}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/60310672\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>) and <em>P</em>(<em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub>) quantities, i.e. the photometric-redshift distribution and the photometric-<em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub> distribution, are calculated using the cross-validation technique described in Rydberg et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). For each <em>z</em> or <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub>, the model (for the considered grid) with the highest cross-validation value (i.e. quality of fit) is selected. A good quality of fit for a certain grid thus means that there is at least one model in the grid that fits the observations well. The cross-validation value is normalized to the interval [0, 1] (where 1 implies that each data point is perfectly reproduced by the model), and is used as a proxy for a probability distribution.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, each row corresponds to one of the three objects Abell 2261-9000, Abell 2261-14000 and Abell 2261-1366, as well as the stacked version, Abell 2261-stacked. The observational data and spectrum of the best-fitting model for each grid are listed in the first column, while the second column shows the <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>) from different model grids.</p>                        <a id=\"60310675\" scrollto-destination=\"60310675\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/467/1/10.1093_mnras_stx157/2/m_stx157fig4.jpeg?Expires=1639262684&amp;Signature=Lqltp6bnBxSv3kzKi75eBYOAgmkYDWSSl8YyIZyhcJaTrGP47b9RVMf8~FpmGmueqsVPQ1GeunsvPN9-Y~giMgI9-CHNMyOuTc1kM8aS5rCDJmpUobaGK3ysQoc4Tn0mesxo0tp81-LdVYmIXS20K8DpwnOuY2B4Wnkk-FVLuN6~nHG~B8WsRbM067M6aNzKkaTSbf~DBe~sQ8-mxD0gP-ZW8iFjwIYZBQdITR07koXKJChBDn6vhbLSHVPbup9dTBuo70ehTo1Z~3EATxzi3VFAUQxJxgIaQBG6lQtiKij4EDwsz-NMT~na8MWC0N9J10gu36OE4Swr5O-RmSTQPg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Left-hand column: magnitudes and synthetic spectra of the best-fitting model for each grid. Limiting magnitudes are shown in dark grey for 2\u03c3\u20133\u03c3 detections and light grey for 3\u03c3\u20135\u03c3 detections. Right-hand column: P(z) plots comparing the four grids of models. The model with the best quality of fit for each z is shown. All three images and the stacked image exhibit a Ly\u00a0\u03b1-break in the best-fitting models in the range of \u03bb\u00a0\u223c\u00a08000\u201310\u2009000\u00a0\u00c5. All three images and the stacked image exhibit a Ly\u00a0\u03b1-break in the best-fitting models in the range of \u03bb\u00a0\u223c\u00a08000\u201310\u2009000\u00a0\u00c5. The Pop III grid for Abell 2261-1366 has a higher Ly\u00a0\u03b1-break and hence an even higher photometric redshift, but the Yggdrasil grid has a slightly better quality of fit. The large uncertainties in the redshift estimate for Abell 2261-1366 can be seen in the right-hand column. The other two images and the stacked image have significantly smaller uncertainties.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310676 id=\"60310676\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5-6>5.6 Constraints from non-<em>HST</em> observations</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">To rule out old and/or dusty objects, data from four <em>Spitzer</em> filters were examined (Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2-1\"></span><a href=\"#sec2-1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">2.1</a>). Visual inspection yielded no detections in these filters. This strengthens the case for the high-redshift solutions since low-redshift dusty (<em>E</em>(<em>B</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>V</em>)<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn4\" data-open=\"fn4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>4</sup></a> \u2273 0.3, when dust attenuation is implemented on Yggdrasil models) objects would be visible in the <em>Spitzer</em> 3.6- and/or 4.5-mm filters. For objects at the same <em>z</em> as the photometric redshift of our object, both extremely dusty objects and old galaxies would be visible and thus are ruled out.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Extremely strong optical emission lines (such as [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] lines) from low-redshift objects can affect broad-band photometry SED fitting and mimic a high-redshift Ly\u00a0\u03b1 line (Huang et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). But since there is continuum data for this candidate at longer wavelengths and it registers as a non-detection at wavelengths shorter than the Ly\u00a0\u03b1-break, it is likely not due to a strong non-Ly\u00a0\u03b1 emission line from a faint object. Huang et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) has conducted a search for extremely strong emission-line galaxies of this type in CLASH and our candidate is not among those they identified, further strengthening the conclusion that our results are not due to strong optical emission lines. Furthermore, our spectroscopic observations, which show no prominent detections in the <em>J</em> band (Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">2.2</a>), disfavour strong [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] or [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] from objects at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a02.1\u20134.8.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310679 id=\"60310679\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5-7>5.7 Counter-image comparison</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">When considering different images as counter-images of the same object, the images have to be consistent with regard to redshift as well as morphology and fluxes (including magnification).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">As a first step, we analyse Abell 2261-stacked (Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5-5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5-5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">5.5</a>). We find a best fit at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.3, with good quality fits extending from <em>z</em><sub>L</sub> \u2248 5.6 to <em>z</em><sub>U</sub> \u2248 6.5 and EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) \u2248160 \u00c5. The most plausible redshift is 6.3 for the Yggdrasil Z\u00a0&gt;\u00a00 grid as well with essentially equally good fits. Hence, there are alternatives to the Pop III interpretation of the object. The Gissel and CWW, Kinney grids are not considered further because their <em>P</em>(<em>z</em>) are significantly lower than those of the Yggdrasil grids. Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a> contains the quality of fits dependence on <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub> for Abell 2261-stacked at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.3. It is evident that both Yggdrasil grids have a strong dependence on <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub>.</p>                        <a id=\"60310682\" scrollto-destination=\"60310682\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/467/1/10.1093_mnras_stx157/2/m_stx157fig5.jpeg?Expires=1639262684&amp;Signature=bQB~NagZ94eE1PPA8raltoJKTaAaGHNM6OlqwQTsv8ZFi22JrNgc8NFZ1WC44gFCmsmsRZrHQoVEz3k2bMM4V4rIbt-p9wBZOjzyBmdPZj~Hgkz6MSbJZlObdW0OS~5Ud-r~jGcq6EjM53llqWSd7TWi3tnxU7YSFzXchaYQy9-ttNiMeNoufUCL4CL7CI83V0gTRyGr3nJrKSADNnYRTWsHSudsoR5ku5mQlzZA4aIsLIfFjLNLzYHNHSp0hwD8UtI0~KBKTcy1cMTdvX7wS06osJjhK-vN9IXi2~s8mql36kT-Jh31VPrX075RFxD9huaTyFbMqFh1m1P8W5z6aQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The quality of fit as a function of the Ly\u00a0\u03b1 escape fraction fLy\u2009\u03b1 at z\u00a0=\u00a06.3. It has a strong dependence on <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub>. The Pop III model grid allows a lower <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub> with a high quality of fit. This could mean that there is a (admittedly weak) case for it to be metal-free, or at least have a very low metallicity.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a> compares the magnitudes of the three counter-images after de-lensing them using our magnification estimates. We see that Abell 2261-14000 is approximately 1.0\u00a0mag brighter (a factor of 2.5) than Abell 2261-9000 and Abell 2261-1366 after de-lensing. But the errors in the magnification estimates, which are systematic across the filters, are large and the discrepancies are within the errors. The observations in <em>F850LP</em>, however, have too large a difference to be explained by magnification errors. Also, when comparing the colours in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>, the observations in the IR filters are consistent with an SED with a declining slope, while only the <em>F850LP</em> observation for Abell 2261-9000 is consistent with this SED. The <em>F850LP</em> magnitudes of Abell 2261-14000 and Abell 2261-1366 are much brighter and fainter, respectively. This poses a challenge for the interpretation but it could possibly be explained by an extended Ly\u00a0\u03b1 halo around the galaxy. The sensitivity of the detector could be such that the halo is only detected if the object is bright enough, and would hence contribute a disproportionate amount of flux if the object is brighter. The flux is consistent with this scenario because it is significantly higher in the brightest image Abell 2261-14000, compared to Abell 2261-9000 and Abell 2261-1366. The reason Abell 2261-1366 differs from Abell 2261-9000 could be that Abell 2261-1366 is contaminated by light from the nearby star, rendering the halo indistinguishable from the illumination provided by the star.</p>                        <a id=\"60310684\" scrollto-destination=\"60310684\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig6\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 6.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/467/1/10.1093_mnras_stx157/2/m_stx157fig6.jpeg?Expires=1639262684&amp;Signature=wXXBWoWHSWvct~M60IBSL~6wz34WIWf1qgjTS68AT17i6QCovRK~yWs5qLmgkoKsP7pCNEkcaqcxJzmzI7IXQFglgMRETgntzXanFqxOVJd4P3uoXi5af9wphEmD8BeDWQSAN1lJXKCI9LCFwJcbxM2ylohu8PN6N3mAOJwhgIpb6Zla~tVVC2yYCX4AqSE9fw912Sql5DXAj1TCEeAi3J9~d4AM99hcqqOzi6-oKHRCTTgEOHZ~a8Jy5su-Y0TVxzb36-z-~oBb3ivZKnhamyU0fM7tB7Qs2TyiBeD22qEYDM7l3Ohn6RxUMczcvFyAa3c25IqlLpK0q9-Pm1IVMA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"AB-magnitudes for the three images de-lensed with our magnification estimates. The observational trends in the other filters, i.e. being fainter with wavelength in IR, holds for the different images. However, Abell 2261-14000 is \u223c1 AB-magnitude lower than the other counter-images, or about a factor of 2.5 brighter. The errors in magnifications are systematic, and large enough to bridge the differences in magnitude between the images.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310685 id=\"60310685\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5-8>5.8 Interacting galaxies?</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">To constitute a merger or a virialized galaxy, the substructure must be within the virialized radius of the DM halo. Hence, if the distance between the two clumps is larger than twice the virialized radius, the two clumps are neither a part of the same object nor in the process of becoming one, even though a future collision might produce a merger. The lowest stellar mass suggested by our models for this structure is \u223c10<sup>6</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub>. If 15\u00a0per\u00a0cent of the halo mass is baryonic and all the baryons reside in stars (in reality, only a small fraction do, which would imply an even more massive halo), the halo would be\u00a0\u223c7\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>6</sup> M<sub>\u2299</sub>. We take the virialized mass of the structure to be <em>M</em><sub>200</sub>, the mass enclosed by the radius at which the density is 200\u00a0times the critical density, which also corresponds to the virialized radius R<sub>200</sub>. For <em>M</em><sub>200</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a07\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>6</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub>, <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0800\u00a0pc. Since we assume the minimum mass for the DM halo, this is the lower limit to its virialized radius.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The angular distances between the centres of substructure in the three images (0.60\u00a0arcsec for Abell 2261-9000, 0.87\u2009arcsec for Abell 2261-14000 and 1.2\u2009arcsec for Abell 2261-1366), which correspond to de-lensed separations of \u2273370\u00a0pc at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.3. Since the distance between the substructures along the sightline is unknown, this should be taken as a lower limit. Comparing this to the minimum virialized radius <em>R</em><sub>200</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0800\u00a0pc, we conclude that the clumps could be close enough to be virialized, and therefore substructure in a galaxy or an interaction/merger.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The proximity of the clumps could indicate a galaxy merger (Lin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>) but could also be a chance projection of two high-redshift objects whose actual separation along the sightline is too large for a merger. However, given the similarities in morphology, estimated redshift and colours between the three images, it is unlikely they could be chance projections. The AB-magnitude increase due to greater separations would have to be compensated by correspondingly lower luminosity. The angular size of the substructures are also roughly equal, which would have to be a coincidence if the substructure were a chance projection. Because our current data cannot constrain collision angle or speed, composition (which we have assumed to be the same in our combined fitting) or relative mass, further investigation is required to determine if the lensed object is a merger.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310689 id=\"60310689\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6>6 SUMMARY AND CONCLUSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We have discovered a likely multiply lensed LAE candidate with three counter-images, Abell 2261-9000, Abell 2261-14000 and Abell 2261-1366, with magnifications <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\mu \\sim 4.3^{+1.6}_{-1.7}{}$|\u2060</span>, <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$3.7^{+1.3}_{-1.3}{}$|</span> and <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$5.5^{+2.4}_{-2.3}{}$|\u2060</span>, respectively. Another image is predicted but is not detected. In the process of locating the counter-images, we measured the first spectroscopic redshift (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.377) of a multiply imaged galaxy behind Abell 2261, which was used to calibrate the lensing model. Using this in combination with the photometric redshifts of our three images, we conclude that it is likely that the three images are of the same object. The LAE was initially considered to be a Pop III galaxy candidate but competitive fits with non-zero metallicity models indicate that other interpretations are equally likely. By combining these fits using Yggdrasil models, a redshift estimate of 6.3 was obtained for the galaxy. This redshift estimate is dependent on the assumption of strong Ly\u00a0\u03b1 emission. Future observations targeting Ly\u00a0\u03b1 in these objects will soon be able to test these predictions.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In two of the counter-images, substructure appearing as two objects in the same orientation expected from the lens model were found in the filters with the longest wavelengths. The substructure could also be discerned in the third image, even though it was not separable into two objects. They could be star-forming regions belonging to the same galaxy or two interacting galaxies. SED fitting of models to photometry implies extreme Ly\u00a0\u03b1 emission, with EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) \u223c160 \u00c5. We also find that the angular distance between its substructures is not too large for them to be a single virialized structure or a merger between two small galaxies. The kinematics of the structures could not be constrained with current observations but might be with additional surveys. Follow-up observations could target any of the lines: [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] (Knudsen et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>), [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (Inoue et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>), [C\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span>] (Stark et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib72\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib72\" data-open=\"bib72\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015b</a>) and/or C\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (Stark et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib71\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib71\" data-open=\"bib71\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015a</a>). Given a detection, splitting in the observed line would indicate large relative velocities that may indicate a recent merger.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">If confirmed through spectroscopy, this galaxy would join a rising number of LAEs now being discovered at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06. Similar objects at higher redshifts may be the birthplaces of direct collapse black holes, the likely precursors of the most massive black holes at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06 (Mortlock et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Wu et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib77\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib77\" data-open=\"bib77\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). Others, as may be true of CR7, may harbour Pop III stars. CLASH, Frontier Fields and future surveys of cluster lenses may reveal these objects and yield clues to the origins of the first stars and supermassive black holes in the Universe.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310693 id=\"60310693\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title js-splitscreen-backacknowledgements-title\" >Acknowledgments</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">CER acknowledges funding from the Swedish National Space Board and the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences. data-legacy-id=\"bib10\"><div class=\"refLink-parent\"><span class=\"refLink\"><a name=\"jumplink-bib10\" href=\"javascript:;\" aria-label=\"jumplink-bib10\" data-id=\"\"></a></span></div><div   <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\r\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310611 id=\"60310611\" class=\"abstract-title js-splitscreen-abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">While the Lyman\u00a0\u03b1 (Ly\u2009\u03b1) emission line serves as an important tool in the study of galaxies at <em>z</em> \u2272 6, finding Ly\u00a0\u03b1 emitters (LAE) at significantly higher redshifts has been more challenging, probably because of the increasing neutrality of the intergalactic medium above <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a06. Galaxies with extremely high rest-frame Ly\u00a0\u03b1 equivalent widths, EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) \u2273 150 \u00c5, at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06, are good candidates for Ly\u00a0\u03b1 follow-up observations, and can stand out in multiband imaging surveys because of their unusual colours. We have conducted a photometric search for such objects in the Cluster Lensing And Supernova survey with Hubble (CLASH), and report here the identification of three likely gravitationally lensed images of a single LAE candidate at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a06.3, behind the galaxy cluster Abell 2261 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.225). In the process, we also measured with Keck/Multi-Object Spectrometer For Infra-Red Exploration the first spectroscopic redshift of a multiply imaged galaxy behind Abell 2261, at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.337. This allows us to calibrate the lensing model, which, in turn, is used to study the properties of the candidate LAE. Population III galaxy spectral energy distribution model fits to the CLASH broad-band photometry of the possible LAE provide a slightly better fit than Population I/II models. The best-fitting model suggests intrinsic EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) \u2248160 \u00c5 after absorption in the interstellar and intergalactic medium. Future spectroscopic observations will examine this prediction as well as shed more light on the morphology of this object, which indicates that it may be a merger of two smaller galaxies.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix at-ArticleMetadata\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;stars: Population III&quot;\">stars: Population III</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: clusters: individual: Abell 2261&quot;\">galaxies: clusters: individual: Abell 2261</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: high-redshift&quot;\">galaxies: high-redshift</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;cosmology: observations&quot;\">cosmology: observations</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;dark ages, reionization, first stars&quot;\">dark ages, reionization, first stars</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310613 id=\"60310613\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Cluster lensing is a powerful tool for detecting and studying astronomical objects in the distant Universe (see Kneib &amp; Natarajan\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>, for a recent review) and has the potential to bring otherwise undetectable objects within the reach of existing or upcoming telescopes (e.g. Ellis et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>; Kneib et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>; Zackrisson et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib78\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib78\" data-open=\"bib78\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib81\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib81\" data-open=\"bib81\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Vanzella et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib73\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib73\" data-open=\"bib73\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Rydberg et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib60\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib60\" data-open=\"bib60\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Bouwens et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Bradley et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Whalen et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib76\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib76\" data-open=\"bib76\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib84\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib84\" data-open=\"bib84\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Atek et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For galaxies at redshifts <em>z</em> \u2273 5, the Ly\u00a0\u03b1 line is one of the few emission lines currently available for the spectroscopic confirmation of photometric candidates (although other alternatives exist; see, for example, Inoue et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>; Stark et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib70\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib70\" data-open=\"bib70\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib86\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib86\" data-open=\"bib86\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015b</a>; Pentericci et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib53\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib53\" data-open=\"bib53\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). However, LAEs are rare in the reionization era (e.g. Hayes et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>), possibly because of the increasingly neutral intergalactic medium at this epoch (for a recent review, see Dijkstra\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). Until recently, no secure Ly\u00a0\u03b1 detections existed above <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a07.5 (Schenker et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib63\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib63\" data-open=\"bib63\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Finkelstein et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), but lately, more than a handful of spectroscopic detections have been reported at <em>z</em> \u2273 7.5 (e.g. Ono et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49\" data-open=\"bib49\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Finkelstein et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Oesch et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib47\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib47\" data-open=\"bib47\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; Watson et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib75\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib75\" data-open=\"bib75\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib87\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib87\" data-open=\"bib87\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015c</a>; Song et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib69\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib69\" data-open=\"bib69\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>; Knudsen et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), with the highest redshift LAE currently known at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a08.68 (Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib87\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib87\" data-open=\"bib87\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015c</a>) and the earliest Lyman break galaxy, whose break was verified further by <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) grism, at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a011.1 (Oesch et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">At somewhat lower redshifts, a fraction of LAEs exhibit very high Ly\u00a0\u03b1 equivalent widths (extreme LAEs, with EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) \u2273 150 \u00c5; e.g. Malhotra &amp; Rhoads\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib45\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib45\" data-open=\"bib45\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>; Kashikawa et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). They are intriguing because they could, in principle, host populations of metal-free (Pop III) stars (e.g. Schaerer\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>; Raiter, Schaerer &amp; Fosbury\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). Other explanations for these extreme LAEs include gas cooling (Dijkstra\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>), directional EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) boosting (Gronke &amp; Dijkstra\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>), accreting black holes (Haiman &amp; Rees\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) and stochastic sampling of the stellar initial mass function (Forero-Romero &amp; Dijkstra\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). Kashikawa et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) present an LAE with an observed EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) of 436<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$^{+422}_{-149}$|</span> \u00c5 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.538. The Ly\u00a0\u03b1 line is spectroscopically measured and the continuum is detected in their deep <em>z</em>-band image. In Sobral et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), two objects, CR7 and MASOSA, were presented, both with EW(Ly\u03b1) \u2273 200 \u00c5 at <em>z</em><sub>spec</sub>\u00a0=\u00a06.604 and 6.541, respectively. To our knowledge, the Kashikawa et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) object and CR7 are the first extreme LAEs detected above <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a06.5. Sobral et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) conclude that CR7 is best explained by one population of mostly Pop III stars and two populations of Pop II/I stars (metallicity Z\u00a0&gt;\u00a00). However, Pallottini et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib52\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib52\" data-open=\"bib52\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) as well as Agarwal et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) and Hartwig et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) argue that the observations are better explained by a direct collapse black hole accreting primordial gas, a possibility also briefly discussed in Sobral et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). Bowler et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) object to both interpretations, claiming that deeper observations in <em>Spitzer Space Telescope</em> (<em>Spitzer</em>) 3.6 and 4.5 micron bands indicate strong [O\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] emission. They suggest two alternative interpretations, either a type II AGN or a low-metallicity starburst.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">A plausible triple galaxy merger at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.595, dubbed \u2018Himiko\u2019, was presented in Ouchi et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib51\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib51\" data-open=\"bib51\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). Although not an extreme LAE, it has strong Ly\u00a0\u03b1 emission with EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) = 78<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$^{+8}_{-6}$|</span> \u00c5. Its Ly\u00a0\u03b1 halo was detected with a narrow- band filter and appears as a \u2018blob\u2019 covering all three galaxies. The authors use ALMA to observe the [C\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] line as it is a tracer of star-forming regions and could potentially reveal kinematics of the merger. However, no line was observed, suggesting that Himiko is a metal-poor object. Huang et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) present a triply imaged galaxy behind the galaxy cluster MACS 2129. Like Himiko, it has similarly strong Ly\u00a0\u03b1 emission with EW(Ly\u00a0\u03b1) = 74 \u00b1 15 \u00c5 and a spectroscopic redshift of 6.85.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In Zackrisson et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib79\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib79\" data-open=\"bib79\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011a</a>), we argue that extreme LAEs could, in principle, be identified from <em>HST</em> broad-band data even at redshifts up to <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a08\u20139 because of their unusual colours. The key point is that the relevant emission lines are so prominent that the broad-band to which the line is redshifted would appear significantly brighter because of the additional flux from the line. Since we know (in part from spectral energy distribution or SED models) that which lines are typically stronger in star-forming young galaxies, we can anticipate the effect of brightening on the broad-band photometry at a given redshift. This selection technique is similar in nature to that recently adopted by Smit et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib66\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib66\" data-open=\"bib66\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib67\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib67\" data-open=\"bib67\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) and Roberts-Borsani et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) to successfully search for <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a06\u20138 galaxies with strong rest-frame optical emission lines. For example, all four objects at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a07 predicted by Roberts-Borsani et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) to be prominent emission-line galaxies from their unusual broad-band colours were later found to exhibit Ly\u00a0\u03b1 in follow-up observations (Oesch et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib47\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib47\" data-open=\"bib47\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; Stark et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib72\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib72\" data-open=\"bib72\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015b</a>; Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib87\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib87\" data-open=\"bib87\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015c</a>; Roberts-Borsani et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The Cluster Lensing And Supernova survey with Hubble (CLASH) survey (Postman et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib55\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib55\" data-open=\"bib55\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) has now produced an extensive broad-band data set for the identification of lensed galaxy candidates at high redshifts (e.g. Zheng et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib82\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib82\" data-open=\"bib82\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Coe et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Bradley et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Vanzella et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib74\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib74\" data-open=\"bib74\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). In a companion paper (Rydberg et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), we presented a search for Pop III LAE candidates at <em>z</em> \u2273 6 in CLASH, which resulted in two singly imaged candidates with best-fitting redshifts at <em>z</em> \u2248 8. These candidates \u2013 if confirmed spectroscopically \u2013 might like the aforementioned Roberts\u2013Borsani objects be the highest redshift extreme LAEs detected so far.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Here we examine a possible multiply lensed object behind the galaxy cluster Abell 2261 observed with CLASH. We use a lens model for Abell 2261, which we revise here following the first spectroscopic measurement of a multiply imaged galaxy behind this cluster. The aim is to establish the object as a viable candidate to be a triple-lensed LAE.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Throughout this paper, we assume a \u039bCDM Universe with cosmological parameters <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a067.3, \u03a9<sub>M</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.308 and \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.692 based on <em>Planck</em>, WP (<em>Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</em> polarization), highL (high-resolution cosmic microwave background) and baryon acoustic oscillation data (Planck Collaboration et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib54\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib54\" data-open=\"bib54\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">2</a>, we review the available CLASH, <em>Spitzer</em><em>Satellite</em> and spectroscopic data sets, and in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">3</a>, we discuss the stellar population models applied in our study. The gravitational lens model we construct is described in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">4</a>. The analysis of the LAE is presented in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">5</a>, and we conclude in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec6\"></span><a href=\"#sec6\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">6</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310622 id=\"60310622\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 OBSERVATIONS</h2>\r\n                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310623 id=\"60310623\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-1>2.1 Imaging</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">CLASH has deep <em>HST</em> imaging data for 25 galaxy clusters, including Abell 2261. The observations of Abell 2261 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.225) cover 2000\u201317\u00a0000 \u00c5 in 16 filters from the UV to the near-infrared. A photometric catalogue for Abell 2261, generated with SE<span class=\"small-caps\">xtractor</span> (Bertin &amp; Arnouts\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1996</a>), was made publicly available as a high-end CLASH product (Postman et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib55\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib55\" data-open=\"bib55\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). SE<span class=\"small-caps\">xtractor</span> was run in dual-image mode with a detection image created through a weighted sum of the IR images. The detection image was specifically created this way to be sensitive to red objects, such as high-redshift galaxies (Postman et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib55\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib55\" data-open=\"bib55\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). Position, aperture and photometry were then determined in the detection image. The result for Abell 2261 was a photometric catalogue containing 2127 potential objects, with data in all 16 filters. The ID numbers for the images of the LAE candidate we use in this study are from this catalogue.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Abell 2261 has also been imaged by <em>Spitzer</em>. We use the publicly available<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a> images from the Infrared Array Camera (Fazio et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>) 3.6-, 4.5-, 5.8- and 8.0-mm filters. The 3.6- and 4.5-mm filters reach \u223c23 AB magnitudes, while the 5.8- and 8.0-mm filters reaches only \u223c20 AB magnitudes. These approximate limits are deduced from 5\u03c3 detections in the official <em>Spitzer</em> CLASH catalogues. The two shorter wavelength filters go deep enough to constrain dust attenuation and age; see Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">5</a>.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310626 id=\"60310626\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-2>2.2 Spectroscopy</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Spectroscopic observations of Abell 2261 were carried out with the Multi-Object Spectrometer For Infra-Red Exploration (MOSFIRE; McLean et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) on the Keck 1 telescope, as part of a search for UV metal lines in <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a07\u20138 lensed objects (described in Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib86\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib86\" data-open=\"bib86\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015b</a>). Abell 2261 was observed on 2014 September 16 for 2.2\u00a0h in the <em>H</em> band in sets of 120-s exposures. An AB dither pattern of \u00b11.25\u2009arcsec along the slit was used and the typical seeing was \u223c0.6\u2009arcsec. All spectroscopic data were reduced with the public MOSFIRE pipeline. For each reduced slit (biased, flat-fielded and combined), the 1D spectrum was then extracted with an 11\u00a0pixel boxcar (\u223c1\u2009arcsec) centred on the target. The 1\u03c3 error was extracted with the same procedure, in quadrature.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The <em>H</em>-band (14\u00a0500\u201317\u00a0770 \u00c5) mask included a slit placed on multiple image 4a at (17:22:28.56, +32:08:07.92), using the ID given in Coe et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). The image was identified initially by the method of Zitrin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib83\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib83\" data-open=\"bib83\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>), which is also the one used here for lens modelling. Coe et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) obtained a photometric redshift of 3.48 \u00b1 0.03 for this galaxy, and the lens models presented therein similarly predict <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a03.3. Our spectroscopic observations, shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>, indicate a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.377 based on the [O\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] (3727, 3729\u00a0\u00c5) doublet and [N\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (3869 \u00c5) line, in good agreement with the photometric and initial lens model prediction. This redshift measurement is important, and will allow us to recalibrate, for the first time, the lens model of Abell 2261. The <em>H</em>-band observations also covered one of the objects presented in this paper,<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a> although no lines were detected.</p>                        <a id=\"60310629\" scrollto-destination=\"60310629\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/467/1/10.1093_mnras_stx157/2/m_stx157fig1.jpeg?Expires=1639262684&amp;Signature=exfJ-oCRHcqCYBy0dJrqM7b2k~mXOWsI15QfXDYSTzbzxLPP6clEX4Wfl04~lIgJe4rAZZiPdT2q5QxqROqYWLXdS7MK4Ku4J8o1OANos5ZyoEeRCbMrWs0SGQi7ugqqzAf-2uBiQsAdCnW01iFTEddUfbF2eUJcwGhPl77IgQrgQVEsYnTF4ZBl1HXXVFRrJfo~bzdeec1pBdRgIgdfX0JvmEGs1lx2QPzz6bmXoYweSSgdK6m4HdclnD6PFxzTUH7ga3ApKI3SyEfwK2Ip5kYyC3au4rCMmuLVmWZM9QR6BbVBJRazfhOAIORiOCEw-1ZfstHGU-e-vmtK84oEVQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"H-band spectrum of a multiply imaged source at z\u00a0=\u00a03.377 lensed by Abell 2261, taken with MOSFIRE on the Keck 1 telescope (see Section\u00a02.2). The upper panel shows the 2D spectrum, whose 1D boxcar extraction is shown in the bottom panel along with a slightly smoothed version and the 1\u03c3 error. Only the central part of the <em>H</em> band appears here, showing the detection of the [O\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] (3727, 3729 \u00c5) doublet and the [N\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] (3869 \u00c5) line at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.377. This redshift is the first one measured for a multiply imaged source in Abell 2261, and it anchors the redshift calibration of the lens model for this cluster.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We have also examined <em>J</em>-band (11\u00a0500\u201313\u00a0520 \u00c5) observations covering two of the objects presented here.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a> They were carried out on 2015 June 10 and 11, for a total of 2.9\u00a0h. No significant lines were detected.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Detections of metal lines in the <em>H</em> or <em>J</em> band could have ruled out the possibility of the galaxy being metal-free. The <em>J</em>-band observations also weaken the plausibility of the object being a low-redshift galaxy; see Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">5</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310632 id=\"60310632\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 SPECTRAL ENERGY DISTRIBUTION MODELS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">To derive various physical properties and the redshifts of galaxies, model spectra are usually fitted to the photometric data. The models can be either synthetic spectra, empirical spectra or a mix of the two. We consider here four different grids of spectra to fit to the observed data.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310634 id=\"60310634\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1>3.1 Yggdrasil</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The Yggdrasil population synthesis model is described in detail in Zackrisson et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011b</a>), so only a brief summary is given here. The Yggdrasil model adopted in this paper features the parameters listed in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>, described in more detail in Rydberg et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>); see also Sections\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3-1-1\"></span><a href=\"#sec3-1-1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">3.1.1</a> and\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3-1-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec3-1-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">3.1.2</a> for <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u03b1</sub> and the initial mass function (IMF). The model is applied in two grids: one Pop III galaxy grid (<em>Z</em> = 0) and one grid with <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\mathrm{{{ Z}}} &gt; 0$|\u2060</span>. The purpose is to compare the quality of fit to the two different grids to investigate whether the observations correspond to a Pop III galaxy or a galaxy containing metals.</p> class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/60310636\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=60310637 id=\"60310637\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1-1>3.1.1 The Lyman\u00a0\u03b1 line</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In Yggdrasil models with nebular emission (i.e. <em>f</em><sub>cov</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00), significant intrinsic Ly\u00a0\u03b1 emission is produced. However, the Ly\u00a0\u03b1 line is highly resonant and prone to scatter in neutral hydrogen. The parameter <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub> represents the fraction of Ly\u00a0\u03b1 photons escaping absorption by dust and scattering out of the line of sight in both the interstellar medium (ISM) and the intergalactic medium (IGM). The equivalent widths (EW) used throughout the paper are rest-frame EWs compensated by <em>f</em><sub>Ly\u2009\u03b1</sub>, so cosmological expansion has not been taken into account.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The Gunn\u2013Peterson trough (Gunn &amp; Peterson\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1965</a>) is approximated by setting the flux below the Ly\u00a0\u03b1 wavelength (\u03bb<sub>Ly\u03b1</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01216\u00a0\u00c5) to zero for all model spectra at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06. At <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a06, we simulate the fractional absorption by neutral hydrogen clouds through use of the model by Madau (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1995</a>).</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=60310640 id=\"60310640\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1-2>3.1.2 Initial mass function</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">It is worth noting that in recent studies of the Milky Way halo stellar populations (Carollo et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>, and references therein), alternative IMFs were considered to take into account the properties of the inner and outer halo populations (Carollo et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and their building blocks.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Carollo et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) analysed the distribution of two main classes of carbon-enhanced metal-poor stars (CEMP-s and CEMP-no; Beers &amp; Christlieb\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) in the two halo components. They found that the outer halo possesses a much larger fraction of CEMP-no stars, while the inner halo has a fraction of CEMP-s, which is twice the fraction of CEMP-no. These two CEMP classes had different progenitors: intermediate-mass stars (1.3\u20133.5\u00a0M<sub>\u2299</sub>) for the CEMP-s, and massive progenitors (10\u201360\u00a0M<sub>\u2299</sub>) for the CEMP-no. Because of such distinct chemical signatures, Carollo et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) argued that it is likely that the outer halo dwarf galaxy progenitors possessed a flatter IMF with a slope \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a0\u22121.5. In contrast, the inner halo dwarf galaxy progenitors had a standard IMF with slope \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a0\u22122.35 (assuming a Salpeter power law).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Population synthesis models that employ the alternative IMFs derived from Milky Way halo studies could impact the mass estimate and the results of our fits, and will be considered in future papers.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310644 id=\"60310644\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-2>3.2 Gissel</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">This is a grid of synthetic spectra from Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). Gissel contains models with different metallicities (but not zero metallicities, i.e. no Pop III stars), ages and extinction. These models do not contain nebular emission. To account for dust attenuation, we use three different attenuation curves (Seaton\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1979</a>; Prevot et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib56\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib56\" data-open=\"bib56\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1984</a>; Calzetti et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310646 id=\"60310646\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-3>3.3 CWW, Kinney</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The CWW, Kinney grid (Coleman, Wu &amp; Weedman\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1980</a>; Kinney et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1996</a>; Arnouts et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1999</a>) builds partly on empirical spectra. The base are UV observations of nearby galaxies that are then extrapolated with the Gissel code. Starburst galaxies with emission lines are included. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec3-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">3.2</a>, we use three different attenuation curves (Seaton\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1979</a>; Prevot et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib56\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib56\" data-open=\"bib56\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1984</a>; Calzetti et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>) to simulate dust attenuation.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310648 id=\"60310648\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 LENS MODEL</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Since our LAE images are lensed by a cluster, we use a lens model to derive their magnification and determine if the images are of the same source. The spectroscopic measurement discussed in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2-2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2-2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">2.2</a> provides the first redshift for a multiply imaged galaxy behind the cluster and thus allows us to calibrate and refine an existing lens model for this cluster (Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib85\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib85\" data-open=\"bib85\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015a</a>). The original model is available from the Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes as a high-end CLASH science product (Postman et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib55\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib55\" data-open=\"bib55\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). We use the light-traces-mass (LTM) approach by Broadhurst et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) and Zitrin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib83\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib83\" data-open=\"bib83\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>,\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib85\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib85\" data-open=\"bib85\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015a</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The basic LTM model is constructed from two mass components. The first mass component corresponds to the contribution of cluster galaxies and the second one is the contribution by dark matter (DM). We start by identifying cluster-member galaxies by following the red sequence in a colour\u2013magnitude diagram. Each galaxy is assigned a power-law mass density distribution that is projected two-dimensionally and scaled by its luminosity. The superposition map of the contributions from all cluster galaxies is then smoothed with a 2D Gaussian kernel to obtain the smooth, light-tracing-mass DM component. The two components are then summed with relative weights and supplemented by a two-parameter external shear. This basic model has six free parameters: the exponent of the galaxy power law, which is the same for all galaxies, the width of the Gaussian kernel, the relative galaxy-to-DM weight, the direction and amplitude of the external shear, and the overall normalization of the model.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The best-fitting parameter values are derived through a several-thousand long Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC), using \u03c7<sup>2</sup> criteria for the reproduced positions of multiple images. Here we generally rely on the list of multiple images presented in (Zitrin et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib85\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib85\" data-open=\"bib85\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015a</a>, see also Coe et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>), but use only as constraints the four most secure systems, fixing the redshifts of systems 4 and 5 to the redshift we measure here for image 4.a. Most systems lack spectroscopic redshifts, which means that they can be freely optimized by the model around their photometric redshift value. To allow for further flexibility in the reproduction of images, we also allow for the mass and ellipticity of the brightest cluster galaxy to be freely optimized in the minimization procedure.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We use a \u03c7<sup>2</sup> multiple-image positional error of 0.5\u00a0arcsec for the minimization, and errors are extracted using 50 realizations of the MCMC model. Note that in Zitrin et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib85\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib85\" data-open=\"bib85\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015a</a>), we quantified that the error maps with this positional uncertainty are likely underestimated, and we combine this positional uncertainty with the systematic uncertainty thus estimated by taking the square root of the squared sum. The systematic uncertainty was found to be more representative of shifts caused by random structure along the line of sight (Host\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) and better encompasses the differences between lens modelling techniques.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We use this model to help us better understand if these are counter-images of the same source, which were not used as constraints. The final model has an image reproduction rms of 0.60\u2009arcsec.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=60310654 id=\"60310654\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>5 RESULTS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Our LAE candidate was identified when searching for Pop III galaxies in the CLASH data. The method, described in more detail in Rydberg et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), uses \u03c7<sup>2</sup> and cross-validation (see Singh\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib65\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib65\" data-open=\"bib65\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1981</a>) to fit the observational data to the four model grids (Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">3</a>). The \u03c7<sup>2</sup>-fitting was carried out with the publicly available <span class=\"small-caps\">lephare</span> code (Ilbert et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>), while cross-validation was done with a program we developed, which was called <span class=\"small-caps\">observational data scanner</span>. The two methods produce very similar results. The CLASH data set was then scanned for objects with good quality of fits to Pop III model galaxies. The fits should also, preferably, be significantly better than galaxy models containing metals. The search also included the criterion that the objects must be sufficiently extended to rule out point sources.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=60310656 id=\"60310656\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5-1>5.1 Abell 2261-910 and Abell 2261-911</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In the Pop III galaxy search, Abell 2261-910 (previously published in Bradley et\u00a0al.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) was initially flagged as a potential Pop III candidate, although its multiply lensed nature was not suspected at the time (Rydberg\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). Although it was later found not to be a convincing Pop III galaxy candidate because the quality of the fit for Yggdrasil <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\mathrm{{{ Z}}}&gt;0$|</span> models was considered to be comparable, its multiply lensed nature was revealed in the analysis. In the region containing Abell 2261-910, a nearby object Abell 2261-911 was identified at a similar redshift and may be a part of the same system, substructure of the same galaxy or a member of an interacting galaxy pair. Because of their proximity, Abell 2261-910 and Abell 2261-911 will be hereafter treated as substructures of a single object, Abell 2261-9000. Therefore, the fluxes in each filter were added and new magnitudes were calculated. This method might differ in the flux an independent measurement would pick up since the aperture would differ. Abell 2261-9000 has 5\u03c3 detections in <em>F850LP</em> and all IR filters, except for a 10\u03c3 detection in <em>F110W</em>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Using the redshifts implied from Yggdrasil fits and the lensing model (Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec js-xref-sec\">4</a>), the expected positions of counter-images for Abell 2261-9000 were then derived. The four positions within the Abell 2261 cluster and the magnification map of the cluster are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>. The coordinates of the other three areas were used to search for counter-images. All objects within 4\u2009arcsec of the predicted counter-image were considered.</p>                        <a id=\"60310659\" scrollto-destination=\"60310659\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/467/1/10.1093_mnras_stx157/2/m_stx157fig2.jpeg?Expires=1639262684&amp;Signature=yxYGwh6xxAmNWS0kWtoJ~KvqWWfQudwqBVwXJAmPgu6ZaDjv-dffi6-6WaCjyyt8R2K9Pej-lLkokh-fXN2VTp17ncq8R0NDo1I75T7n8BlMJrnp7O6~4ig-OsAOaegRnXYhoH6dc730ZLRvW5-sl4QtgpJf2JtkprlILda1u5RZBClwG4kGYz9QWH-8VQCq~oorI4VZOPxo3zCVmMednkwE3hRrEy213c4VBQIvU7Ifw80vNhRXNRY8vxeB3wU3USK4Vjns9ABQWe21VrS3-mcnIJ8w9ZcbxQwIOG-lNJ0hsIrQgga9HtZQyXbWjbfCVxrmC7cqL4QDJbb2pbAPNg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"100\u2009arcsec\u00a0\u00d7\u00a0100\u2009arcsec overviews.
